She has also appeared in documentaries like *Margaret Atwood: Once in August* and *In the Wake of the Flood*. Furthermore, Atwood wrote the screenplay for the 2012 film *Payback*. Her diverse contributions highlight her versatility and enduring influence in literature and media.
Marie Curie, born as Maria Salomea Skłodowska on November 7, 1867, in Congress Poland, was a renowned figure in the fields of Physics and Chemistry. Under the guidance of her doctoral advisor, Gabriel Lippmann, she pursued her academic career, later supervising several doctoral students, including Ladislas Goldstein, André-Louis Debierne, and Émile Henriot. Her influential work impacted numerous individuals, such as Branca Edmée Marques, Gioacchino Failla, Moi Meng Ling, William Duane, and Marguerite Perey, who were all influenced by her contributions. In her personal life, she was married to Pierre Curie and had two children, Ève Curie and Irène Joliot-Curie. Marie Curie passed away on July 4, 1934, in Passy, Haute-Savoie, due to aplastic anemia. Her legacy endures through the Marie Curie charity, which shares her namesake location, continuing her impactful work in science and beyond.

Her academic expertise lay in palaeobotany, a discipline that combines paleontology and botany. In her personal life, Marie was married to Humphrey Verdon Roe and later to Reginald Ruggles Gates. She had a son, Harry Stopes-Roe, with her first husband. Marie's literary contributions include authoring the notable book "Married Love," which was later adapted into a film where she also served as the writer. Charlotte Carmichael Stopes was her parent, highlighting a family legacy that likely shaped her early life and career.

Peter I of Russia was a prominent figure who held significant roles in various military campaigns. He served as the commander during the Pruth River Campaign, the Battle of Grodno in 1708, and the Battle of Lesnaya, showcasing his leadership in multiple conflicts. He had several children, including Peter Petrovich, Grand Duchess Anna Petrovna of Russia, and Grand Duchess Natalia Petrovna of Russia, who was born in 1718 and passed away in 1725.

He is the author of several influential books, including *Animal Liberation*, *The Life You Can Save*, and *One World: The Ethics of Globalisation*. Pierre Trudeau was born in Quebec on October 18, 1919, and passed away in the same province on September 28, 2000. His full name was Joseph Philippe Pierre Yves Elliott Trudeau. He pursued higher education at several institutions, including Sciences Po, Université de Montréal, and Collège Jean-de-Brébeuf. Trudeau had three children: Alexandre, Justin, and Michel. Professionally, he worked as both a jurist and a lawyer. His political career was marked by affiliations with the Co-operative Commonwealth Federation and the Liberal Party of Canada. He was married to Margaret Trudeau. In addition to his political roles, Pierre Trudeau served as the commander of the operation known as "Seven Days to the River Rhine." He preceded Joe Clark's first tenure and succeeded Lester B. Pearson in his roles. During his time as prime minister, he served under the tenures of Kenneth D. Taylor, Roy MacLaren, and Don Jamieson.
Pierre de Coubertin, born as Pierre de Frédy on January 1, 1863, in Paris, France, is renowned for founding the International Olympic Committee (IOC). He pursued his education at Sciences Po, which laid the groundwork for his future endeavors. Coubertin served as the president of the IOC, a position he held with distinction. His tenure was preceded by Demetrios Vikelas and followed by Henri de Baillet-Latour, Sigfrid Edström, and David R. Francis. Tragically, Pierre de Coubertin passed away on September 2, 1937, in Geneva, Switzerland, due to a heart attack. His legacy endures as a pivotal figure in the establishment and growth of the Olympic movement.
Plato was born in Ancient Greece in 428 BCE and died there in 348 BCE. He was influenced by notable figures such as Pythagoras, the Eleusinian Mysteries, and Archytas. Plato is a prominent figure in Western philosophy. Among his notable works are the dialogues "Minos," "Republic," and "Symposium." Manuel Chrysoloras is known for his connection to Plato.
Pope Benedict XVI was born on April 16, 1927, in Bavaria, with the birth name Joseph Aloisius Ratzinger. His father, Joseph Ratzinger Sr., raised him there. He succeeded John Paul II and resided in Vatican City during his papacy. Pope Gregory XIII, born Ugo Boncompagni on January 7, 1502, in Bologna, Italy, was a prominent figure in the Catholic Church. He attended the University of Bologna, where he likely laid the foundation for his future career. Before becoming pope, he succeeded Pope Pius V, who preceded him, and was later succeeded by Pope Sixtus V. During his papacy, he adopted the motto "Aperuit et clausit," which translates to "Opened and closed," reflecting his approach to his role. Pope Gregory XIII is also notable for his personal life; he had a son, Giacomo Boncompagni, highlighting that even in his elevated position, he maintained a family life. His papacy came to an end with his death in Rome on April 10, 1585, marking the conclusion of his tenure. His legacy includes the establishment of the Gregorian calendar, a significant contribution to timekeeping.

Qin Shi Huang, who ruled from 221 BCE, was a significant figure in history. He was the first emperor of a unified China and played a crucial role in the Qin dynasty. Born to Queen Dowager Zhao and King Zhuangxiang of Qin, Qin Shi Huang succeeded his father at a young age. He was known for his leadership in the wars of unification, which he commanded, leading to the consolidation of various warring states under one rule. Qin Shi Huang had two notable sons, Fusu and Qin Er Shi. Following his death, Qin Er Shi succeeded him as emperor, continuing the dynasty. This succession highlighted the transition of power within the imperial family, ensuring the continuation of Qin Shi Huang's legacy.
Rauf Denktaş was born on January 27, 1924, in British Cyprus and passed away on January 13, 2012, in North Nicosia. He was a prominent Turkish Cypriot politician and a member of the National Unity Party. Denktaş served as the Vice President under Makarios III and later held the presidency during the tenures of Mehmet Ali Talat, Hakkı Atun, and Mustafa Çağatay. He was also a key figure as the commander during the Turkish invasion of Cyprus. In his political career, he succeeded Fazıl Küçük and preceded Mehmet Ali Talat and Nejat Konuk in various roles. Personally, he was the father of Serdar and Raif Denktaş. His legacy is marked by significant contributions to the political landscape of Northern Cyprus.

Richard Dawkins, born as Clinton Richard Dawkins on March 26, 1941, in Nairobi, Kenya, is a prominent figure in the fields of evolutionary biology and science communication. He pursued his academic career at the University of Oxford, where he earned both a Master of Arts and a Doctor of Philosophy. His doctoral studies were guided by Nikolaas Tinbergen, a notable ethologist. Dawkins is widely recognized for introducing the concept of memes and advocating for the gene-centered view of evolution. He is also known for his critical perspectives on religion. Throughout his career, Dawkins has received several prestigious awards, including the International Cosmos Prize and the Zoological Society of London award. He is a Fellow of the Royal Society, a distinction that underscores his significant contributions to science. His work has been influenced by thinkers such as Christopher Hitchens, W.D. Hamilton, and his mentor, Nikolaas Tinbergen. Dawkins has authored numerous influential books, including "An Appetite for Wonder," "The Ancestor's Tale," and "The Root of All Evil?." He founded the Richard Dawkins Foundation for Reason and Science, which he is also a key person of. In addition to his literary contributions, he has been involved in various documentaries, such as "The Root of All Evil?" and "The Unbelievers," and has written and starred in "Break the Science Barrier." On a personal note, Dawkins has been married to Lalla Ward and Marian Dawkins. He has also mentored several notable scholars, including Alan Grafen and Mark Ridley. His work continues to be a significant influence in both scientific and public discourse.

Sigmund Freud, born as Sigismund Schlomo Freud on May 6, 1856, in the Austrian Empire, was a prominent figure in the fields of psychoanalysis, neurology, and psychotherapy. He passed away on September 23, 1939, in Hampstead. Freud's academic journey began at the University of Vienna, where he laid the foundation for his future contributions. His work was recognized with esteemed awards, including those from the Royal Society and the Goethe Prize. Freud's intellectual influences were diverse, drawing from the works of Schopenhauer, Josef Breuer, and Empedocles. He is renowned for his theories on psychoanalysis, repression, and the Oedipus complex. In his personal life, Freud was married to Martha Bernays, and they had two children, Ernst L. Freud and Anna Freud. Anna Freud followed in her father's footsteps, becoming a notable figure in psychoanalysis herself. Among his notable works are "On Narcissism," "Beyond the Pleasure Principle," and "Introduction to Psychoanalysis," which remain foundational texts in the field. Freud's legacy endures through his contributions to psychology and the continued relevance of his theories in understanding human behavior.

Stephen Hawking was born in Oxford on January 8, 1942, and passed away in Cambridge on March 14, 2018. He pursued his academic career under the guidance of Dennis W. Sciama and later became a doctoral advisor to notable physicists such as Christophe Galfard, Gary Gibbons, and Bernard Carr. His work primarily focused on general relativity and quantum gravity. Hawking was married to Jane Hawking and had a child, Lucy Hawking. He influenced several individuals, including Sasha Haco, Christophe Galfard, and Werner Israel. Steven Weinberg, a prominent figure in theoretical physics, was born on May 3, 1932, and passed away on July 23, 2021, in Austin, Texas. His academic journey was guided by Sam Treiman, who served as his doctoral advisor. Weinberg, in turn, mentored notable physicists such as John Preskill and Edward Tryon. His contributions to the field are marked by significant works, including the Joos–Weinberg equation, the Electroweak interaction, and the concept of the Axion. Weinberg's achievements were recognized with several prestigious awards, including the Breakthrough Prize, the Andrew Gemant Award, and the Elliott Cresson Medal. Beyond his scientific endeavors, he was a prolific author, penning influential books like "Cosmology," "Lake Views: This World and the Universe," and "The First Three Minutes." His personal life included marriage to Louise Weinberg, and he also made an appearance in the film "Nobelity." Werner Karl Heisenberg, born on December 5, 1901, in the German Empire, was a prominent figure in the realm of theoretical physics. He passed away on February 1, 1976, in Bavaria. His academic journey began at the University of Göttingen and continued at the University of Munich, where he was guided by the esteemed Arnold Sommerfeld. Heisenberg's contributions to science were profound, as he is renowned for the canonical commutation relation, quantum field theory, and quantum fluctuation. His work extended beyond academia; he authored "The Physical Principles of the Quantum Theory," a seminal text in the field. Heisenberg's influence was not limited to his research. He mentored several notable scholars, including Reinhard Oehme, Edward Teller, and Friedwardt Winterberg. His impact also reached beyond his direct students, influencing figures such as Jane Dewey and Manfred Eigen. Recognized for his achievements, he received prestigious awards like the Barnard Medal, Pour le Mérite, and Matteucci Medal. On a personal note, he was a father to Jochen and Martin Heisenberg. His legacy endures through his contributions to physics and the scholars he inspired.
